<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_74302b79-4912-42c4-8bbe-e8262279cc87'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10746410599791249167">Patricia Highsmith's story of romantic obsession may be one of the most important, but still largely unrecognized, novels of the twentieth century. First published in 1952 and touted as "the novel of a love that society forbids," the book soon became a cult classic.<br/><br/>Based on a true story plucked from Highsmith's own life, <i>Carol</i> tells the riveting drama of Therese Belivet, a</span>
  <span id="freeText10746410599791249167" style="display:none">Patricia Highsmith's story of romantic obsession may be one of the most important, but still largely unrecognized, novels of the twentieth century. First published in 1952 and touted as "the novel of a love that society forbids," the book soon became a cult classic.<br /><br />Based on a true story plucked from Highsmith's own life, <i>Carol</i> tells the riveting drama of Therese Belivet, a stage designer trapped in a department-store day job, whose routine is forever shattered by a gorgeous epiphany—the appearance of Carol Aird, a customer who comes in to buy her daughter a Christmas toy. Therese begins to gravitate toward the alluring suburban housewife, who is trapped in a marriage as stultifying as Therese's job. They fall in love and set out across the United States, ensnared by society's confines and the imminent disapproval of others, yet propelled by their infatuation. Carol is a brilliantly written story that may surprise Highsmith fans and will delight those discovering her work.<br /><br />This authorized edition includes an afterword by Patricia Highsmith. Previously titled <i>The Price of Salt</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10746410599791249167" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Just realised all involve couples where one half is younger, but none of these novels are YA. Hope this helped! :)<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a href="/questions/524070-i-just-finished-it-because-i-will"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I just finished it (because I will probably never get to see the movie) and I'm a big Patricia Highsmith fan anyway. <br /><br />But I have a question: I totally missed the significance of the paining. Can someone elucidate for me? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/50970809-catlick"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/50970809-catlick">Catlick</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">In the historical context of the novel, the 'pain[t]ing' might have been a nod to the existing Freudian view of the neurotic mechanisms that skewed th<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">In the historical context of the novel, the 'pain[t]ing' might have been a nod to the existing Freudian view of the neurotic mechanisms that skewed the development of a 'normal' sexual orientation from the innate. In the absence of a loving mother figure, Therese had an idealized image of the portrait to aspire to. She perhaps internalized this image, and it became part of her attraction map, (if you will). Her attraction to Carol was no less real as a result of this, but the recognition shocked her. Highsmith was constantly exploring images and ideas of what attracts and repels: Mrs Robicheck's plump dry aging hands with their remnant red polish and cheap rings, one with a "clear green stone' versus Carol's strong hands, red lacquered nails, and clear green sapphire ring.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerFooter'>

</div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119772817" />
    <a title="S" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1790227-s"><img alt="S" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/119772817?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S" href="/user/show/1790227-s">S</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119772817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5582978646488211677"> Slinky 1950's couture, lesbian chic, unfiltered cigarettes and bottomless highballs have reappeared in the American zeitgeist and perhaps that style cycle is responsible for this sleek creature finally clawing its way out of confinement. It saddens me to think this book has been stuffed into a musty box labeled "lesbian romance" and left to molder for over fifty years. <br/> It is a dark and forceful account of erotic obsession. It is a terrifying fairy tale told beside a phalanx of glass-eyed dolls</span>
  <span id="freeText5582978646488211677" style="display:none"> Slinky 1950's couture, lesbian chic, unfiltered cigarettes and bottomless highballs have reappeared in the American zeitgeist and perhaps that style cycle is responsible for this sleek creature finally clawing its way out of confinement. It saddens me to think this book has been stuffed into a musty box labeled "lesbian romance" and left to molder for over fifty years. <br /> It is a dark and forceful account of erotic obsession. It is a terrifying fairy tale told beside a phalanx of glass-eyed dolls. It picks you up in the middle of a tantrum and plops you down in a theater set where the beautiful sparkling ice queen feeds you oranges and warm milk and then tucks you into bed without a kiss. It is the sunlit long drive when you had mommy all to yourself and you sat in the front seat and she told you how much she loved you and you knew you were her favorite. It is perfectly reasonable to believe that Nabokov's imagination was shaped by this book. <br /> I'll never lock eyes with a stranger again without wisps of this story seeping up under my mental doors and floating into awareness.Perhaps the novel's pivotal, transformative energy is the real reason why so many were so eager to slip it into a box with a reductive label and stow it away in our collective attic.<br /><br /><br /> <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5582978646488211677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText14700014771057178237" style="display:none">Ok. I have Feelings about this book. And there might be some spoilery things, but no more than I was spoiled before reading it, so...it's probably not too bad.<br /><br />I spent a large part of this being depressed because Carol's a total dick to Therese most of the time. HOWEVER. Omg the ending. Basically the last 20 or so pages. Awesome. And who doesn't love a road trip book? Because this is two ladies in love WHO ROAD TRIP IT. In the '50s. In America. Like Lolita, but less child-rapey. (I would like this to be the blurb on the book, please)<br /><br />Highsmith has an afterword in the edition I read, that she wrote in '89, saying (among other things, of course): "Prior to this book, homosexuals male and female in American novels had had to pay for their deviation by cutting their wrists, drowning themselves in a swimming pool, or by switching to heterosexuality (so it was stated), or by collapsing—alone and miserable and shunned—into a depression equal to hell."<br /><br />So THANK YOU, HIGHSMITH, for starting the trend of fixing that. And in the totally repressive year of 1953! So awesome.</span>

<div id="review_916264185" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/916264185" />
    <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi"><img alt="Glenn Sumi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444333567p2/28854579.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/916264185?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Sumi" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi">Glenn Sumi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28854579-glenn-sumi?shelf=1900-1960">1900-1960</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer916264185" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8800644141869496560"><b>UPDATED, December 3, 2015:</b> <i>Just saw <b>Carol</b>, the Todd Haynes film adaptation starring Cate Blanchett and Rooney Mara. Gorgeous looking, and very faithful to the book. The cinematographer captures the era beautifully, and Haynes plays a lot with windows and reflections in an effective way. Therese's profession has been changed from budding set designer to budding photographer, which works well for a visual medium. The two leads are terrific, and Mara particularly makes you understand this character</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8800644141869496560" style="display:none"><b>UPDATED, December 3, 2015:</b> <i>Just saw <b>Carol</b>, the Todd Haynes film adaptation starring Cate Blanchett and Rooney Mara. Gorgeous looking, and very faithful to the book. The cinematographer captures the era beautifully, and Haynes plays a lot with windows and reflections in an effective way. Therese's profession has been changed from budding set designer to budding photographer, which works well for a visual medium. The two leads are terrific, and Mara particularly makes you understand this character who tends to be a passive watcher. I preferred Far From Heaven, but the tone was much different. This movie isn't a big blockbuster, of course, but rather a handsome arthouse flick. </i> <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426163295i/13982850.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Published in 1952 under the pen name Claire Morgan, this book by the author of the Ripley novels and <i>Strangers On A Train</i> chronicles the love between 19-year-old set designer Therese and the wealthier, older and more worldly suburban mom Carol. <br><br>The lesbian plot might feel slightly tame today, and Therese comes across as overly passive (Highsmith addresses this in the afterward). But the novel, Highsmith's second, is well-structured, sensitively written (at times it's almost claustrophobic in its details) and an intriguing portrait of 50s repression and conformity.<br><br>Furthermore, the book's said to have influenced Nabokov's own tale about forbidden love, <i>Lolita</i>, and the Oscar-winning women-on-the-run film <i>Thelma &amp; Louise</i>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426163393i/13982878._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>For years <i>The Price Of Salt</i> was relegated to the suspense and thriller shelf. But now, rightly so, it's being recognized for its literary merit. A couple of scenes sizzle: in the women's first meeting, Therese, who's working part-time in a department store before Xmas, helps out Carol, who's looking for a doll, and the two obviously share a connection; and there's a terrifically catty scene later on between Therese and Carol's nosy (and possibly also lesbian) friend Abby.<br><br>Director Todd Haynes, who brought 50s-style hidden homosexuality so evocatively to the screen in <i>Far From Heaven</i>, is directing a forthcoming adaptation of the book (called <i>Carol</i>), with Cate Blanchett (see production pic above) and Rooney Mara in the leads. Can't wait. <br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="8800644141869496560" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2444846560" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32804270-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564311252p2/32804270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2444846560?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/32804270-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=recs">recs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2444846560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5816709154578049944">A foreboding and atmospheric tale about love between women, <i>The Price of Salt</i> sensitively portrays an aspiring set designer’s coming to terms with her sexuality. Set against the backdrop of postwar repression, the story follows nineteen-year-old Therese Belivet as she abandons her quiet life as a shopgirl for a budding romance with an older, married lover, Carol Aird. The bulk of the novel’s drama arises from Carol’s fraught attempt to divorce her affluent husband, retain custody of her daughter</span>
  <span id="freeText5816709154578049944" style="display:none">A foreboding and atmospheric tale about love between women, <i>The Price of Salt</i> sensitively portrays an aspiring set designer’s coming to terms with her sexuality. Set against the backdrop of postwar repression, the story follows nineteen-year-old Therese Belivet as she abandons her quiet life as a shopgirl for a budding romance with an older, married lover, Carol Aird. The bulk of the novel’s drama arises from Carol’s fraught attempt to divorce her affluent husband, retain custody of her daughter, and build a life with Therese; class conflict between the two women counterpoints the main plot and saves the relationship from feeling sappy. In hardboiled prose, Highsmith fully renders the heroine’s inner life, her sense of apprehension and self doubt, and the author expertly captures the young artist’s near-obsessive reverence for Carol as well as her resentment toward her wealth. The novel’s aged well and benefits from rereading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5816709154578049944" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_567455845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/567455845" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2262926-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420907415p2/2262926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/567455845?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/2262926-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2262926-maria?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2262926-maria?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer567455845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10262808858586141765">I should be asleep by now... I even turned off the lights! I just couldn't, though, I just couldn't stop thinking. The first word that comes to mind after reading this novel? <i>Odd</i>. This was my first Highsmith's book and she has quite a personal writing style. It's different... but you find yourself going with the strange flow of words. I can't believe this was written in the 50's. The ending is so... bittersweet! I am still rather lost in it... Their relationship? It just <i>happens</i>. I must confess </span>
  <span id="freeText10262808858586141765" style="display:none">I should be asleep by now... I even turned off the lights! I just couldn't, though, I just couldn't stop thinking. The first word that comes to mind after reading this novel? <i>Odd</i>. This was my first Highsmith's book and she has quite a personal writing style. It's different... but you find yourself going with the strange flow of words. I can't believe this was written in the 50's. The ending is so... bittersweet! I am still rather lost in it... Their relationship? It just <i>happens</i>. I must confess that at first I couldn't quite understand why Therese felt so drawn to Carol. I mean, I understood why she was drawn to her in the first place, but why she kept coming back after the many strange <i>meetings</i> with Carol? Now that I didn't quite understand. Not until I learned more about Therese, that is. Then it all seemed to fall into place. It was love. It was life. It was everything. Yes, I am completely aware of the fact that I sound like a hopeless romantic. But you know what? I don’t care.<br /><br />There’s one thing I find extremely interesting about this book. Usually, when you are reading a novel about a romantic couple, you are driven to fall in love with both characters yourself, whether they are both female, male, or one of each. This novel is different, though. You are a witness to this relationship, not part of it. You forget they are two women, you forget how cold Carol seems to be and how Therese seems to be more obsessed with Carol than anything else. And guess what? At the end of the book you just can’t help it. You find yourself smiling and you feel happy for them. You fall in love with them... <i>together</i>. Two human beings who found love. How brilliant is that?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10262808858586141765" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1410061581" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1410061581" />
    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3845138-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438649960p2/3845138.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1410061581?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/3845138-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3845138-carol?shelf=book-group-choice">book-group-choice</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3845138-carol?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1410061581" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5172459601231948150"> Salt, as defined by Merriam-Webster: “…. <i>an ingredient that gives savor, piquancy, or zest</i>”….; or, as it relates to this story, the price (sacrifice) these women paid to live their lives truthfully (hence, the book title, I’m guessing). I admired Highsmith’s nerve and honesty for tackling this lesbian love story in the time period when it was so obviously taboo.<br/><br/>Therese Belivet is a young and struggling set designer working in a department store when she meets and instantly becomes enamored with</span>
  <span id="freeText5172459601231948150" style="display:none"> Salt, as defined by Merriam-Webster: “…. <i>an ingredient that gives savor, piquancy, or zest</i>”….; or, as it relates to this story, the price (sacrifice) these women paid to live their lives truthfully (hence, the book title, I’m guessing). I admired Highsmith’s nerve and honesty for tackling this lesbian love story in the time period when it was so obviously taboo.<br /><br />Therese Belivet is a young and struggling set designer working in a department store when she meets and instantly becomes enamored with Carol Aird, a sophisticated and wealthy married woman. The meeting leads to a relationship that causes Therese to mature as well as some foreseeable repercussions for the married mother, Carol.<br /><br />Therese’s growth and transformations are subtle and nuanced. Initially, she is very naive, vulnerable and almost obsessively smitten with the older Carol. She later matures into a confident young woman with a sympathetic grasp of who Carol is and what she may be going through. The author captures the ecstasy (and agony) of an intense new love beautifully. It’s a thoughtful character study and erotic in a romantic sense rather than with explicit sex.<br /><br />Lesbian literature is often suggested for my book group; and, even though there have been more than a few excellent choices (such as those written by Sarah Waters) many have been sub-par (pulp-like) fiction. It was enjoyable to read a novel where lesbian characters are so well-written with powerful descriptions of an intense new love that rings emotionally true.<br /><br />The film adaptation is called "Carol" and coming soon to my theatre. I can’t wait!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5172459601231948150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1124106368" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1124106368" />
    <a title="BrokenTune" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune"><img alt="BrokenTune" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1124106368?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BrokenTune" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BrokenTune" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune">BrokenTune</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1124106368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16663363242736181674"><i>‘Don’t you want to forget it, if it’s past?’<br/>‘I don’t know. I don’t know just how you mean that.’<br/>‘I mean, are you sorry?’<br/>‘No. Would I do the same thing again? Yes.’<br/>‘Do you mean with somebody else, or with her?’<br/>‘With her,’ Therese said. The corner of her mouth went up in a smile.<br/>‘But the end was a fiasco.’<br/>‘Yes. I mean I’d go through the end, too.’<br/>‘And you’re still going through it.’<br/>Therese didn’t say anything.</i><br/><br/>Patricia Highsmith got the idea for <i>Carol</i> (or <i>The Price of Salt</i> as it was named originall</span>
  <span id="freeText16663363242736181674" style="display:none"><i>‘Don’t you want to forget it, if it’s past?’<br />‘I don’t know. I don’t know just how you mean that.’<br />‘I mean, are you sorry?’<br />‘No. Would I do the same thing again? Yes.’<br />‘Do you mean with somebody else, or with her?’<br />‘With her,’ Therese said. The corner of her mouth went up in a smile.<br />‘But the end was a fiasco.’<br />‘Yes. I mean I’d go through the end, too.’<br />‘And you’re still going through it.’<br />Therese didn’t say anything.</i><br /><br />Patricia Highsmith got the idea for <i>Carol</i> (or <i>The Price of Salt</i> as it was named originally) shortly after her first novel, <i>Strangers on a Train</i> was published. She lived in New York at the time, was depressed, and in need of money. She took a job as a sales assistant in a department store and, one day, met a lady customer in a mink coat. The stranger in the store made such a strong impression on her that it gave her an idea for a new book. An onset of fever (from chickenpox) shortly after the encounter helped with the writing. <br /><br />I have no idea if the fever really had anything to do with the writing or whether this is just my impression but the story of Therese Belivet and Carol Aird had a feverish quality that had me hooked from the start and had me lose sleep because I had to know how the story would end. Yes, this was another one of those books where I had to stay up all night to finish it, even though the two protagonists were difficult to like at times.<br /><br />Therese is in her early twenties (I think), stuck in a dead-end sales job, has aspirations of becoming a stage designer, and generally seems to lack empathy for any of the people around her. Carol, on the other hand, is a relatively well-off divorcee who gives off an air of detachment. It is only in the course of their story that we get to see behind the veneer that both characters put up for different reasons<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ - one out of immaturity and one out of a need for self-defence <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />However, likable characters is not what Highsmith's books are about. For me, Highsmith's books are primarily about one thing - intensity. This is the aspect that has appealed to me most in her novels. And although the plot and thematic focus of Carol depart from the thriller genre that her publishers wanted her to follow, there is certainly enough "suspense" writing to have kept me reading until the wee hours. In particular, there are two scenes, where I sat on the edge of my seat: one in the later part of the book where I found myself yelling at Therese because she was behaving so childish it drove me mad, and one that had me glued to page thinking that if it were a scene in a film, the theatre audience would collectively gasp and fall silent to see what happens next<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[:<br /><i><br />"‘Crawl in the back and get the gun,’ Carol said.<br />Therese did not move for a moment.<br />Carol glanced at her. ‘Will you?’<br />Therese did agilely in her slacks over the seat back, and dragged the navy blue suitcase on to the seat. She opened the clasps and got out the sweater with the gun. ‘Just hand it to me,’ Carol said calmly.<br />‘I want it in the side pocket.’<br />She reached her hand over her shoulder, and Therese put the white handle of the gun into it, and crawled back into the front seat. The detective was still following them, half a mile behind them, back of the horse and farm wagon that had turned into the highway from a dirt road.<br />Carol held Therese’s hand and drove with her left hand. Therese looked down at the faintly freckled fingers that dug their strong cool tips into her palm. ‘I’m going to talk to him again,’ Carol said, and pressed the gas pedal down steadily</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>."<br /><br />This scene alone is one of the reasons I really want to see the film version and I am miffed that I didn't get a chance to see it at our local cinema. <br /><br />I know that a few readers have found the book slow moving and boring, but I kinda liked the understated pace. It added to the feel of a 1950s road trip into the middle of nowhere, which, I thought, was also an appropriate metaphor for the relationship between Therese and Carol - a journey that lacked company, landmarks, or sign posts. <br /><br />In the Afterword (written in 1989) of the edition I read, Highsmith wrote that she <i>"like[s] to avoid labels. It is American publishers who love them."</i><br />As mentioned above, after the publication of her first novel, <i>Strangers on a Train</i>, Highsmith's publishers wanted to see her establish herself in the thriller genre. They rejected her manuscript of <i>Carol</i> and urged her to write another thriller. Defying her publisher's request, Highsmith offered to release the book under an alias and sought out another publisher who would to publish a lesbian romance novel that dared to criticise contemporary American society in 1952. <br /><br />Considering that this could have been the end of a writing career that had not even started, yet, and considering that presumably there would also have been some backlash to her personal exposure, I truly admire Highsmith's insistence on getting the book published. <br /><br />The publication itself is not the only break with commercial wisdom that happened with<i> Carol</i>. Highsmith also broke with the convention of how she described her characters as ordinary women, how she re-evaluated the importance of home life and family, and asked the specific question of what price people would pay to even attempt living a life of their own design. As such, I must admit that I actually preferred the book's original title: <i>The Price of Salt. </i> <br /><i><br />"In the middle of the block, she opened the door of a coffee shop, but they were playing one of the songs she had heard with Carol everywhere, and she let the door close and walked on. The music lived, but the world was dead. And the song would die one day, she thought, but how would the world come back to life? How would its salt come back?"</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="16663363242736181674" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1395360331?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ty" href="/user/show/38042245-ty">Ty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1395360331" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4262081492735388032">I've tried and tried and tried to understand why people like these two characters and their story so much. I've tried to come to it with an open mind and eyes ready to see whatever it is everyone else sees. But I just cannot seem to do it. I can't read Therese as anything but a petulant child with an obsessive fixation on someone she barely knows. I don't understand the swooning over Carol when, to me, she's written so nebulously that it's almost as if she isn't even present in the novel, let al</span>
  <span id="freeText4262081492735388032" style="display:none">I've tried and tried and tried to understand why people like these two characters and their story so much. I've tried to come to it with an open mind and eyes ready to see whatever it is everyone else sees. But I just cannot seem to do it. I can't read Therese as anything but a petulant child with an obsessive fixation on someone she barely knows. I don't understand the swooning over Carol when, to me, she's written so nebulously that it's almost as if she isn't even present in the novel, let alone present in the relationship with Therese. I find both of them (in the book) wholly unlikeable. <br /><br />This was distressingly hard to read. I remember being insulted when I heard people felt the on the road/camping portion of Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows dragged for them. Impossible, I thought! Well, now it's my turn. The road trip portion of this book was painfully slow to get through. <br /><br />The movie is so much better than the book, in my opinion, though the characterization problems are still present. I think I could have understood so many people having such a positive reaction to it because it is so well made. But the number of people who have called it life changing, and swoon over it? Well, this movie has started to make me feel more and more disconnected from everyone around me. Now I not only don't understand it, I resent it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4262081492735388032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2870599035" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2870599035" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2870599035?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2870599035" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9479408689810540866">Patricia Highsmith lives in an ugly world. Her first novel, <i>Strangers on a Train,</i> is almost unbearably bleak. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1418995955' title='My review' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ripley</a> is...well, it’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1541755177' title='”My' rel='nofollow noopener'>a lot of things,</a> but mainly it’s the first time someone wrote an entire series of books asking you to identify with a serial killer. But between them all, and under a fake name, she also wrote this beautiful, aching jewel of a love story. Who knew?<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561421428i/27721013._SX540_.jpg' alt='highsmith-2' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>Highsmith</i><br/><br/>Not the public, not for thirty years. Astonishingly, Highsmith didn’t take credit for <i>The Price of Salt</i> until </span>
  <span id="freeText9479408689810540866" style="display:none">Patricia Highsmith lives in an ugly world. Her first novel, <i>Strangers on a Train,</i> is almost unbearably bleak. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1418995955" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Ripley</a> is...well, it’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1541755177" title="”My" rel="nofollow noopener">a lot of things,</a> but mainly it’s the first time someone wrote an entire series of books asking you to identify with a serial killer. But between them all, and under a fake name, she also wrote this beautiful, aching jewel of a love story. Who knew?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561421428i/27721013._SX540_.jpg" alt="highsmith-2" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Highsmith</i><br><br>Not the public, not for thirty years. Astonishingly, Highsmith didn’t take credit for <i>The Price of Salt</i> until 1984. She didn’t want to be known as a “lesbian writer.” The book lurked along as a cult classic until then. It did this one silly, revolutionary thing, which unfortunately I can’t say without a big old <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[: it allowed Carol and Therese a happy ending. Most gay novels were required, believe it or not, to punish their protagonists, as in the contested and sorry ending of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1431458268" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Spring Fire,</a> another early lesbian classic. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561421428i/27721015._SY540_.jpg" alt="carol" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Carol</i><br><br>The other revolutionary thing it did, weirdly, is that Highsmith somehow wrote Cate Blanchett. She wouldn’t be born for 17 years but Carol is definitely her, in all her architectural beauty and her ungraspability. She’s slippery and oblique, hard to pin down. Therese, only dimly aware that she’s gay, throws herself at Carol early on and is more or less ignored. She describes Carol as the Winged Victory of Samothrace at one point, which is funny because <br><br><a href="https://imgbb.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561421428i/27721014.jpg" alt="winged-victory" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>it has no head. Carol's tricky. But when she appears in the department store where Therese works, she tears through her world - heretofore as dismal as <i>Strangers</i> - and Highsmith lets her book explode with hope and life. It never really comes back down. Highsmith is one of our best writers, and this is her best book. <br><br>Highsmith was anguished about her own queerness; her characters confuse passion with murder fairly regularly. She fought and hid herself. "Persistently," <a href="http://thisrecording.com/today/2011/8/15/in-which-patricia-highsmith-endures-a-depression-equal-to-he.html" title="This is such a wonderful article about her" rel="nofollow noopener">she said,</a> "I have the vision of a house in the country with the blond wife whom I love, with the children whom I adore, on the land and with the trees I adore. I know this will never be." She couldn't live it, but she could almost write it.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="9479408689810540866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7992238737875698545">This book had me in pieces by the end. That last chapter, <i>oh my god.</i><br/><br/>Never mind the notion of Patricia Highsmith as an "unloving and unlovable woman"-- she clearly understood the painful delicate aches of love and loving and, having lost, the bittersweet triumph in growing up.   <span id="freeText7992238737875698545" style="display:none">This book had me in pieces by the end. That last chapter, <i>oh my god.</i><br /><br />Never mind the notion of Patricia Highsmith as an "unloving and unlovable woman"-- she clearly understood the painful delicate aches of love and loving and, having lost, the bittersweet triumph in growing up. The Price of Salt carries an emotional honesty that is exquisite and devastating.<br /><br />Highsmith's prose is simple but she realizes even the smallest moments with a keen observance. The results are gorgeous and tender, and at times even comic -- the melodramatic angst of interruptions, to the wistfulness in seeing a lover's hands, to the thrill of meeting for the first time... or again.<br /><br />The romance being between two women should really hold no bearing of anyone's enjoyment of this book, but having read the afterword and knowing the climate during the time of publication makes the story seem especially courageous. Swoon! <br /></span>

<div id="review_268970034" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/268970034" />
    <a title="Catherine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1274745-catherine"><img alt="Catherine" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/268970034?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catherine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catherine" href="/user/show/1274745-catherine">Catherine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer268970034" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4821305496297494321">THIS REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS!!!!!<br/><br/>ALERT!!!! BIG BIG SPOILERS!!!<br/><br/><br/><i>The Price of Salt</i>, published in 1952, is considered the first book—and the only one for a very long time afterwards—to depict a lesbian relationship with a happy ending. Having just reread it, what strikes me now is how anyone, even lesbians, especially lesbians, could have thought that losing custody of your child with no visitation rights and being publicly humiliated in court and in the newspapers constituted a happy ending.<br/><br/>But we</span>
  <span id="freeText4821305496297494321" style="display:none">THIS REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS!!!!!<br /><br />ALERT!!!! BIG BIG SPOILERS!!!<br /><br /><br /><i>The Price of Salt</i>, published in 1952, is considered the first book—and the only one for a very long time afterwards—to depict a lesbian relationship with a happy ending. Having just reread it, what strikes me now is how anyone, even lesbians, especially lesbians, could have thought that losing custody of your child with no visitation rights and being publicly humiliated in court and in the newspapers constituted a happy ending.<br /><br />But we said, “At least neither of them died or went to jail or was committed to a mental institution or gave up her beloved to live a ‘normal’ life married to a man.” Because that was what happened to lesbian lovers in pulp fiction. And pulp fiction was the only place you could find stories about lesbian lovers.<br /><br />When I puzzled over the title, <i>The Price of Salt</i>, what first occurred to me was a quote from the book of Matthew in the New Testament:<br />	<i>… if the salt should lose its savour, wherewith shall it be salted?</i><br />I later learned from a biography of Patricia Highsmith that that is indeed where the title came from. In asking the price of salt, she is asking what price a person must pay to live an authentic life. In those days it was a high price, if it was possible at all.<br /><br />At the end of the book Carol and Therese have a future together, and for us that was enough. So <i>The Price of Salt</i> does not have a happy ending, but it does have a hopeful one. And I am hopeful that the release of the movie will herald a time when no one, no matter how ‘different’, must settle for a life unsalted, without <i>savour</i>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4821305496297494321" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2817642935" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2817642935" />
    <a title="Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell"><img alt="Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584062476p2/2190064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2817642935?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell" href="/user/show/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell">Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11430661-the-price-of-salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell?shelf=lgbtqia-library">lgbtqia-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell?shelf=you-can-watch-it-on-the-telly">you-can-watch-it-on-the-telly</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2817642935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16296362084500784660"><a href='http://readasaurus.blogspot.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459070464i/18565492.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><a href='https://www.instagram.com/neniagram/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Instagram</a> || <a href='https://twitter.com/NeniaCampbell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Twitter</a> || <a href='https://www.facebook.com/aficionenias/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Facebook</a> || <a href='http://www.amazon.com/Nenia-Campbell/e/B00AWVRKMS' rel='nofollow noopener'>Amazon</a> || <a href='https://www.pinterest.com/mightbeamisprin/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pinterest</a><br/><br/><br/>THE PRICE OF SALT is an important book in the LGBT+ literary canon because it was one of the few exceptions in the sea of <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lesbian_pulp_fiction' rel='nofollow noopener'>lesbian pulp fiction</a> published during the 50s and 60s where neither of the heroines ended up married off, dead, or institutionalized. No matter how dull or lackluster it seems today, one can only imagine how many feathers were ruffled - especially since many of these authors were explicitly told that in order to avoid</span>
  <span id="freeText16296362084500784660" style="display:none"><a href="http://readasaurus.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459070464i/18565492.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><a href="https://www.instagram.com/neniagram/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> || <a href="https://twitter.com/NeniaCampbell" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> || <a href="https://www.facebook.com/aficionenias/" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> || <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Nenia-Campbell/e/B00AWVRKMS" rel="nofollow noopener">Amazon</a> || <a href="https://www.pinterest.com/mightbeamisprin/" rel="nofollow noopener">Pinterest</a><br><br><br>THE PRICE OF SALT is an important book in the LGBT+ literary canon because it was one of the few exceptions in the sea of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lesbian_pulp_fiction" rel="nofollow noopener">lesbian pulp fiction</a> published during the 50s and 60s where neither of the heroines ended up married off, dead, or institutionalized. No matter how dull or lackluster it seems today, one can only imagine how many feathers were ruffled - especially since many of these authors were explicitly told that in order to avoid censorship, the lesbian characters <b>could not have a happy ending</b>. Was this book banned? Probably. But it's far more enjoyable to think about all the young (and old) women who read this book and felt validated.<br><br><br><br>I wanted to read this book after watching Vox's amazing video, <i><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Rl22caUj-oo" rel="nofollow noopener">Why so many queer female characters die on TV</a>. </i>The video delves into the whole "<a href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Main/BuryYourGays" rel="nofollow noopener">Bury Your Gays</a>" trope, and was a response to Lexa's death on the popular TV show, <i>The 100</i>, and why it's <a href="https://www.themarysue.com/lexa-bury-your-gays/" rel="nofollow noopener">so harmful</a>. In the video, Vox traces the Bury Your Gays trope back to the lesbian pulp fiction period, and cites THE PRICE OF SALT, and its movie adaptation, <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt2402927/" rel="nofollow noopener">Carol</a>, as a rare example of a happy ending in the genre.<br><br><br><br>Therese designs cardboard sets and works in the toy section of a department store. She's dating a dilettante painter who's basically using his hobby as a way to run away from his daddy. One day, a well-dressed and impressive woman named Carol purchases a doll from her counter, and Therese is moved to write her a personalized card, telling her what a pleasure it was to service her (heh). The two of them end up in a relationship, one that starts out as a mentor/friendship affair and quickly becomes something more, propelled by Therese's obsessive and youthful fascination.<br><br><br><br>This being the time period that it was, though, neither Carol's husband nor Therese's boyfriend are quite willing to let them go, and it isn't long before their romantic getaway is spoiled by a hired detective who tries to use proof of their illicit affair as leverage to block Carol from seeing her child. It seems like THE PRICE OF SALT is going to take the usual turn, and force the women onto the correct and virtuous path of heterosexual salvation - but that's where things get interesting.<br><br><br><br>I wanted to like this book a lot more than I did. I know this author can write - I loved her <i>Mr. Ripley </i>series and its gleefully sociopathic antihero. This is a very different style of story-telling and I'm not sure it ages quite as well. The plot is slow and plodding, and Therese is a hard narrator to like. She's very whiny and self-centered, and a bit of a hypocrite, too. During one of her whine-sprees, she's complaining about how Richard, her boyfriend, feels entitled to her, because he was the first to sleep with her and naturally assumes that this means marriage. Granted, this is annoying, classic Chad the Incel behavior, and it's disgusting - but then she kind of does the same thing with Carol, seeming to assume that she's the first woman Carol's been with and getting upset and jealous when she finds out that there's been others, even though it's clear that these relationships aren't ongoing anymore.<br><br><br><br>I think it's worth reading THE PRICE OF SALT because it's a pretty famous and notable example of the genre and did something ground-breaking back in the day. That said, it wasn't really for me, and I don't think this is an example of the writer at her best, sadly - and if you're looking for tawdry or cheap thrills, THE PRICE OF SALT is not particularly erotic or licentious. There are walk-in freezers that are more sensual than these two characters. Maybe that's a constraint of the times, maybe not.<br><br><br><br>2 to 2.5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="16296362084500784660" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/206886673?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bandit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bandit" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit">Bandit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer206886673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11136108442699780170">If you read enough books, you're bound to become jaded once in a while by all the sub par ones out there and then a book like The Price of Salt comes along to remind you just how great a book can really be and what a reading experience should really be like. Yes, I loved this book THAT much :)<br/>My only other experience with Highsmith's work until then has been through Ripley movies and I liked the character of Ripley, but not enough to track down the books. I picked up this book, because the cover</span>
  <span id="freeText11136108442699780170" style="display:none">If you read enough books, you're bound to become jaded once in a while by all the sub par ones out there and then a book like The Price of Salt comes along to remind you just how great a book can really be and what a reading experience should really be like. Yes, I loved this book THAT much :)<br />My only other experience with Highsmith's work until then has been through Ripley movies and I liked the character of Ripley, but not enough to track down the books. I picked up this book, because the cover said that this was the one to inspire Lolita, one of my all time favorites. <br />This was the author's second book, originally published under a pseudonym to avoid being typecast as a lesbian writer. It was also for years misfiled as a mystery, which it really is not. It's a coming of age story, a story about relationships and consequences, a story about a road trip, an eye opening and fairly frightening account of how lesbians were treated as recently as 60 years ago, but most of all it's a really great love story, about first love, the most difficult of all. <br />I've read that Patricia Highsmith has been a particularly unpleasant person, particularly during her later years, which makes it so interesting how well and sympathetically she is able to describe the inner turmoils and thoughts and actions of her characters. I'd say in this book her writing shows great humanity. Of course, I am now interested in reading her other works, though I am not even a mystery fan, just to experience more of that amazing writing.<br />I can't recommend this book enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11136108442699780170" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1418925019" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1418925019" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1418925019?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781504647533" href="/book/show/29407570-carol">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jenny (Reading Envy) by: </span>
      Mayumi and the Sea of Happiness
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2016">read2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1418925019" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2414356251639888779">I wanted to read this before watching the movie ("Carol"), but I first found out about it from the fictional librarian in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23399029.Mayumi_and_the_Sea_of_Happiness' title='Mayumi and the Sea of Happiness by Jennifer Tseng' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mayumi and the Sea of Happiness</a>. From the marketing of this title I could not tell if it was more of a love story or a thriller, and knowing Patricia Highsmith I held my breath through the entire book waiting for someone to turn into a sociopath. <br/><br/>This novel is set in the early 1950s, a time where not many women were openly involved in relationships with one another, leaving T</span>
  <span id="freeText2414356251639888779" style="display:none">I wanted to read this before watching the movie ("Carol"), but I first found out about it from the fictional librarian in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23399029.Mayumi_and_the_Sea_of_Happiness" title="Mayumi and the Sea of Happiness by Jennifer Tseng" rel="nofollow noopener">Mayumi and the Sea of Happiness</a>. From the marketing of this title I could not tell if it was more of a love story or a thriller, and knowing Patricia Highsmith I held my breath through the entire book waiting for someone to turn into a sociopath. <br /><br />This novel is set in the early 1950s, a time where not many women were openly involved in relationships with one another, leaving Therese without a framework and Carol with a choice - Therese or her daughter. Originally Highsmith published it under a pen name and did not own up to being the author of the book for at least twenty years. In the 1950s there were several lesbian pulp romance novels, but the accepted mode was that because of their "unacceptable" choices, someone had to end up with something tragic happening as "punishment." The original cover includes the tagline "the novel of a love society forbids," which would make you think the focus of the story is the two women against the world. It really isn't, it's far more subtle, two women figuring out their relationship within the typical conflicts of previous relationships and ongoing commitments. As romance goes, this was pretty realistic.<br /><br />Discussed even more on <a href="http://readingenvy.blogspot.com/2016/02/reading-envy-052-man-with-eyebrows.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Episode 052 of the Reading Envy podcast.</a></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1025420030" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1025420030" />
    <a title="Mel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5866692-mel"><img alt="Mel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359228708p2/5866692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1025420030?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mel" href="/user/show/5866692-mel">Mel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408808979" href="/book/show/10117779-carol">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=lesbian">lesbian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=20th-century-fiction">20th-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=pulp">pulp</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=library2014">library2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=queer">queer</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1025420030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8272010890864459819">OMG this book had the DULLEST LESBIANS EVER!!! I have read a lot of early queer pulp and normally I love them for their honesty and the raw emotion they present. Here there was none of that, everything just felt like it was being written about behind a veil. There was none of the soul searching and the camaraderie that is found in other lesbian pulps. The writing style did nothing for me either, I felt it was very dry. I almost felt like someone had described being in a queer relationship to Pat</span>
  <span id="freeText8272010890864459819" style="display:none">OMG this book had the DULLEST LESBIANS EVER!!! I have read a lot of early queer pulp and normally I love them for their honesty and the raw emotion they present. Here there was none of that, everything just felt like it was being written about behind a veil. There was none of the soul searching and the camaraderie that is found in other lesbian pulps. The writing style did nothing for me either, I felt it was very dry. I almost felt like someone had described being in a queer relationship to Patricia and she decided to write about that based on her fantasies rather than personal experience. <br /><br />I think the biggest problem I had with this was that Carol and Therese was totally boring. Carol was a rich housewife who did nothing. Therese wanted to have a career but spent her entire time moping expecting her boyfriend's connections to find her work. The two just seemed obsessed with money and shopping and having a very occasional cocktail. Even when they went on their "road trip" there was nothing interesting. There was no sense of adventure or place anywhere they visited. It was just a series of dull hotels. It made you wonder why they bothered to leave New York in the first place.<br /><br />There are so many better old lesbian novels out there. I really can't recommend this one at all. Read Ann Bannon, Thereska Torres or Valerie Taylor instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8272010890864459819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3276852171?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486800295" href="/book/show/23639241-the-price-of-salt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3276852171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15483719262626718522">I haven’t seen the movie <i>Carol</i> (based on this book) but, while reading this, I found it impossible to not see Cate Blanchett as Carol: perfect casting. I also couldn’t help thinking of the novel in cinematic terms during a tense scene in the middle of the novel, one involving a detective; empty U.S. highways; and a gun. I don’t believe Highsmith wrote her novels with films in mind, but they do seem to make good ones.<br/><br/>Speaking of the gun, once it was introduced, I kept thinking of Chekhov all thro</span>
  <span id="freeText15483719262626718522" style="display:none">I haven’t seen the movie <i>Carol</i> (based on this book) but, while reading this, I found it impossible to not see Cate Blanchett as Carol: perfect casting. I also couldn’t help thinking of the novel in cinematic terms during a tense scene in the middle of the novel, one involving a detective; empty U.S. highways; and a gun. I don’t believe Highsmith wrote her novels with films in mind, but they do seem to make good ones.<br /><br />Speaking of the gun, once it was introduced, I kept thinking of Chekhov all through the rest of that road trip. I don’t know if the gun was introduced to thwart expectations, but it did create suspense, and compulsive reading.<br /><br />I don’t mean to imply this is a thriller. It’s not that at all. It’s more a character study of the young woman whose viewpoint we see everything from, of her emotional awareness as she ages from nineteen to twenty-one. Notably, the men in Therese’s life call her Terry (perhaps I noticed that because that’s never been my nickname); the women do not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15483719262626718522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
