  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1469249288?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/5143619-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/28173889-carol">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5143619-michael?shelf=five-star-books">five-star-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5143619-michael?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1469249288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5201489359922290335"><i>How was it possible to be afraid and in love, Therese thought. The two things did not go together. How was it possible to be afraid, when the two of them grew stronger together every day? And every night. Every night was different, and every morning. Together they possessed a miracle.</i><br/><br/>Some of you may remember back to my review of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23731883.The_Book_of_Lost_and_Found' title='The Book of Lost and Found by Lucy Foley' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book of Lost and Found</a> when i boldly declared "barring something truly amazing coming along later this year, I am happy to declare this exceptional read my book of t</span>
  <span id="freeText5201489359922290335" style="display:none"><i>How was it possible to be afraid and in love, Therese thought. The two things did not go together. How was it possible to be afraid, when the two of them grew stronger together every day? And every night. Every night was different, and every morning. Together they possessed a miracle.</i><br /><br />Some of you may remember back to my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23731883.The_Book_of_Lost_and_Found" title="The Book of Lost and Found by Lucy Foley" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Lost and Found</a> when i boldly declared "barring something truly amazing coming along later this year, I am happy to declare this exceptional read my book of the year." Well ladies and gentlemen something truly amazing did come along in the form of this beautiful story that held me in it's gentle embrace from start to finish. <br /><br />First published in 1952 under the pseudonym Claire Morgan, Carol centers on two female characters who are both trying to escape from there past. Therese Belivet is a lonely young women in a relationship with a man she does not love. She wants nothing more than to further her career as a theatre set designer, but for now is working in a department store. It will be during one of her shifts that her life will be transformed when a beautiful and sophisticated older women called Carol Aird comes to her counter. When Carol gives Therese her address to have her purchases delivered, Therese will send Carol a Christmas card that will be the start of their courtship.<br /><br />The two will begins to spend time together and when Carol asks if Therese would like to go with her on a road trip, their relationship will hit a magical crescendo. Over the coming weeks it becomes clear to each other the feelings they have are both romantic and sexual. Carol's husband Harge, who she is locked in divorce proceedings with tries to use emotional black mail to stop the two seeing each other. After being made aware of a letter found at Carol's house, written by Therese declaring her love for Carol, Harge threatens to stop her seeing their young daughter indefinitely unless she ends the relationship and comes back home to New York. For Carol this is a heartbreaking decision to decide between the women she loves and her only child. Will there love survive?<br /><br /><i>Carol raised her hand slowly and brushed her hair back, once on either side, and Therese smiled because the gesture was Carol, and it was Carol she loved and would always love. Oh in a different way now, because she was a different person, and it was like meeting Carol all over again, but it was still Carol and no one else. It would be Carol, in a thousand cities, a thousand houses, in foreign lands where they would go together, in heavan and in hell.</i><br /><br /><br />This book had all the ingredients for amazing read that I am happy to say when combined made for something truly special. The themes are plenty with forbidden love, drama a plenty, humor and perceptions of a by gone time. The fact that the author needed to publish under a different name shows how much of a risk it was to write such a story. I can only imagine what stir this would of caused in the 1950's. The ending for one was revolutionary as it provided something new instead of the depressing conclusions that was the norm with homosexual literature at the time. Both Therese and Carol are also boldly unconventional and the story is so much better for it. If anyone is thinking about reading this wonderful story then I say to you "don't think, do"<br /><br />Ps, I cant wait to see the movie starring Cate Blanchett and Rooney Mara in January and hope it is faithful to the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5201489359922290335" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Even knowing pretty much what happens, there are some differences to the movie... and of course the book is always better! <br/><br/><br/><i>(2019 classic book of the month for July)</i></span>

<div id="review_1346496374" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1346496374" />
    <a title="Jem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18517965-jem"><img alt="Jem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419776659p2/18517965.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1346496374?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jem" href="/user/show/18517965-jem">Jem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18517965-jem?shelf=rich-girl-poor-girl">rich-girl-poor-girl</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18517965-jem?shelf=falling-for-older-women">falling-for-older-women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1346496374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14286345377508097574">Let's get this out of the way first: I'm only reading this book because of Cate Blanchett, and not because it's an outstanding literary work of fiction, which it is. Sorry but I'm hopelessly stuck in the 'lowly' lesbian romance genre. 8-)<br/><br/>Anyway, who can possibly miss all the buzz about the upcoming movie adaptation, especially one with Ms. Blanchett in it. But I remember the last time I watched an f/f movie or Tv adaptation before I read the book (Fingersmith)--the glances, the blank stares and </span>
  <span id="freeText14286345377508097574" style="display:none">Let's get this out of the way first: I'm only reading this book because of Cate Blanchett, and not because it's an outstanding literary work of fiction, which it is. Sorry but I'm hopelessly stuck in the 'lowly' lesbian romance genre. 8-)<br /><br />Anyway, who can possibly miss all the buzz about the upcoming movie adaptation, especially one with Ms. Blanchett in it. But I remember the last time I watched an f/f movie or Tv adaptation before I read the book (Fingersmith)--the glances, the blank stares and the very understated British performances left me cold--it was only after I read the book that I realized what the characters were supposed to be feeling at various points in the story. So, book first before movie. <br /><br />The book is from the point of view of Therese, a 19-year old budding set designer who starts the book moonlighting as a department store saleslady. A chance meeting with a striking woman customer--over the proverbial 'exchange of looks'--sparks what appears to be an obsession for Therese. She is delighted when the interest seems mutual. But the mysterious, alluring Carol is married and has a child. And how does she explain this sudden infatuation with a woman, when Therese has a boyfriend and male admirers in the wings and neither Carol nor Therese herself fit Therese's idea of a lesbian. It's the 1950s so one can just imagine Therese's confusion.<br /><br />The author's prose is incredibly powerful and evocative. From the first few Chapters where Therese feels trapped in her hopeless situation, to the confusion brought about by the tingling sensation of Carol's mere presence, to the exhilarating freedom of the road trip, her despair at Carol's domestic problems, to her growing maturity at the end...all vividly portrayed by Ms. Highsmith's powerful imagery. She is a master at 'showing', never needing to 'tell' us how her characters are supposed to be feeling. Roonee Mara who plays Therese, has her work cut out for her. :) <br /><br />The Price of Salt comes across to me as a bit uneven. The best scenes are of course, every time Carol and Therese interact. They sizzle. Well, Carol does mostly. Since Therese behaved more like a love-struck puppy. The rest of the time, I was just a tad bored and couldn't wait to get to their next encounter. This is due, no doubt, to my over-indulgence in genre romance. :) The rest of the book chronicles Therese's life away from Carol--her claustrophobic existence pre-Carol, her attempts to understand her sexuality (in 1950s style), and post-Carol development as she grows from eager-to-please, compliant admirer and finally comes into her own as Carol's equal. <br /><br />Rating: 4 stars for enjoyment, 5 stars for the writing</span>
  <a data-text-id="14286345377508097574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1173910533" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1173910533" />
    <a title="Agirlcandream" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16738961-agirlcandream"><img alt="Agirlcandream" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361841776p2/16738961.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1173910533?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agirlcandream" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agirlcandream" href="/user/show/16738961-agirlcandream">Agirlcandream</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16738961-agirlcandream?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1173910533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15266290484989135775">I've been listening to the Price of Salt, a literary lesbian classic. The writing is spare and emotions are masked. Therese is innocence trapped within an old soul. She knows her heart and she recognizes in Carol all that has been missing from her life.<br/><br/>Carol is worldly and not easy to like. All that sophistication yet she is drawn to a teenage store clerk and wannabe set designer. They couldn't make an odder couple and yet. And yet. The scenes and dialogue between these too are so vibrant, so do</span>
  <span id="freeText15266290484989135775" style="display:none">I've been listening to the Price of Salt, a literary lesbian classic. The writing is spare and emotions are masked. Therese is innocence trapped within an old soul. She knows her heart and she recognizes in Carol all that has been missing from her life.<br /><br />Carol is worldly and not easy to like. All that sophistication yet she is drawn to a teenage store clerk and wannabe set designer. They couldn't make an odder couple and yet. And yet. The scenes and dialogue between these too are so vibrant, so double edged that the rest of the world they are travelling through falls away. Secondary characters like Richard and Harge seem pale and lifeless. Therese's world without Carol is a cold and grey place.<br /><br />There were moments, especially in the first half of the novel when you feel like you are watching a rather dry play put on by some local actors. <br />Patience by the reader is rewarded with a technicolor ride and I suppose this contrast is what impressed me the most about this novel. <br />Much like the Wizard of Oz, Therese and her life are transformed when she recognizes and accepts who she is and who she loves. The writing is gorgeous. I had to stop listening to the novel and slow down and read the words on the page just to savour Highsmith's talent. She captures that incredible rush one feels when you fall in love for the first time.<br /><br /> "How was it possible to be afraid in love, Therese thought. The two things did not go together. How was it possible to be afraid, when the two of them grew stronger together every day? And every night. Every night was different, and every morning. Together they possessed a miracle." pg. 173<br /><br />And this;<br /><br /> " Once they came upon a little town they liked and spent the night there, without pajamas or toothbrushes, without past or future, and the night became another of those islands in time, suspended somewhere in the heart or in the memory, intact and absolute." pg 177<br /><br />Big sigh. Picturing Cate Blancett and Roona Mara in the starring roles of the soon to be released Carol? Wow. Can't wait. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15266290484989135775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1917198786" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1917198786" />
    <a title="Monica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4739465-monica"><img alt="Monica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577896647p2/4739465.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1917198786?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monica" href="/user/show/4739465-monica">Monica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11430661-the-price-of-salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=betterthanexpected">betterthanexpected</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=female-author-fic">female-author-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=wh_fem_fic">wh_fem_fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=women-savvy">women-savvy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=random-adds">random-adds</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=kindle_fiction">kindle_fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1917198786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10472347455374862238">I picked up this book for Read Harder 2017 read an LGBT romance novel. OK, so romance books are not my genre of choice. In fact they are my least favorite category of books. But this novel billed as a lesbian romance is neither and both at the same time. It is the opposite of this (which in my understanding is the standard of LGBT romance genre): <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1502150584i/23529589._SY540_.png' width='250' height='250' alt='plot cliches' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>It's more a novel about people growing up, learning to love, and choosing to live their life on their terms. This is probably one of the more empoweri</span>
  <span id="freeText10472347455374862238" style="display:none">I picked up this book for Read Harder 2017 read an LGBT romance novel. OK, so romance books are not my genre of choice. In fact they are my least favorite category of books. But this novel billed as a lesbian romance is neither and both at the same time. It is the opposite of this (which in my understanding is the standard of LGBT romance genre): <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1502150584i/23529589._SY540_.png" width="250" height="250" alt="plot cliches" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>It's more a novel about people growing up, learning to love, and choosing to live their life on their terms. This is probably one of the more empowering novels I've read this year. And by the way, there is plenty of romance in the novel as well, but like other romance novels (that I like) the substance is far larger than that.<br><br>What makes this book written in 1952 so special is that it is one of those books that stands up to the test of time. Highsmith is very smart and writes beautifully, poignantly and presents views that prescient and avant garde. This book was ahead of its time with character outlooks and points of view that seem contemporary. This is the story is about two women, Therese and Carol, who meet by chance in a department store at Christmas time. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Nineteen year old Therese is instantly smitten by the sight of Carol. She wants to know more about her. Therese does not identify as lesbian. In fact it doesn't seem to cross her mind. She is dating a young man (Richard) who thinks he is in love with her and she knows that she doesn't love him. Highsmith goes into great detail about their relationship through their numerous encounters throughout the book. While reading this at first the extent of their back and forth seemed rather silly and vacuous. But they are completely necessary towards building an understanding of what's going on in Therese's mind. Therese is 19 and in 1952 things for her should for the most part be settled. She is at the marrying age and Richard thinks they belong together. He knows there is something not right with the relationship, but she has slept with him so he thinks they are on the marriage path. Therese knows there is something wrong too, but just thinks Richard is not the one for her. Therese has other male friends that appeal to her and she sometimes tries to imagines herself with them, but she comes to the conclusion that none of them are for her. Therese also has a strained, estranged, almost nonexistent relationship with her mother. She is captivated by Carol and sends a Christmas card to her along with her order from the store. Carol intrigued by the act decides to thank her in person by taking her to dinner. A bit of a dance begins with Carol inviting Therese to come over for the weekends and to various lunches and drinks after work. Through these meetings we see Carol through the eyes of Therese. A story told via chain smoking, whiskey and brandy. Early on Therese sees her as a woman who wants for nothing. The reader immediately sees a huge chain smoker who puts on airs and pretenses to the public. A poser. But as the story progresses we see a profoundly unhappy woman in the middle of a bitter, ugly divorce battle involving the custody of her daughter Rindy. Carol suppresses a lot of her emotions. She is always on. Always performing. Someone like Therese doesn't really have the life experiences to see beyond the show at first. But the more they meet, the more she sees. Highsmith really is quite wonderful at peeling back the layers. We don't get quite the view of Carol's husband (Harge) as we do of Richard, but in my view Highsmith does a nice job of not creating cardboard villains. All the characters have nuance. Carol's account is that they fell out of love. Somewhere along the marriage she stopped loving him. There wasn't an event etc. Carol does have a history of lesbian encounters including one with her best friend Abby who doesn't appear to have gotten over Carol. The divorce is about to be finalized when Carol decides she wants to travel cross country and she wants Therese to go with her. Road trip!! A couple of days into the trip they become lovers. Therese is completely in love and Carol while still melancholy, seems to be falling too. They experience a few weeks of togetherness before they discover that a detective is following them. Carol confronts him and finds out he is working for Harge and has enough evidence for him to get sole custody of her daughter. Carol goes back to New York for the divorce proceedings but leaves Therese in Iowa with the intent of rejoining her after the hearing. A couple of months go by with Carol sort of half leading Therese on and half "ghosting" her. Finally Carol asks explains that she lost the custody of Rindy and that in order to even see her, she has to eschew Therese and that "lifestyle". Carol's life is completely controlled by Harge. His hostage is Rindy. Therese tries to move on with her life in New York without Carol. Carol asks her to lunch and tells her that the divorce is final and that since the divorce she has taken a job and wants Therese to move in with her. After everything that Carol has put her through, Therese balks. She attends a party and notices her attractions are to females. She tries to envision a life without Carol and seems to realize that there is that possibility…that she can have a life without Carol and be OK. But she also knows that she is still very much in love with Carol. The end of the novel has the two deciding to be together. It is a bittersweet ending because by choosing to be with Therese, Harge can legally bar Carol from ever seeing Rindy. Therese views it as Carol choosing her over Rindy. In my mind Carol was not choosing Therese over Rindy, she was freeing herself literally from Harge and metaphorically from the burdens that society would impose upon her.<br><br>One thing that I loved about the novel is that neither character seemed burdened by traditional conventions or perceptions of the time period or even by current standards. Perhaps unrealistic, but there was no drama associated with their mutual attraction to each other. There is no handwringing about who they are. No tearful proclamations like "I'm a lesbian! Why has the world forsaken me?!?" It's a story about a couple dealing with life as it comes at them. It feels like a book about people breaking the conventional chains that bind them. The conflicts in these women's lives stem from being women; not from being women who love women. Though one could easily extrapolate Harge being resentful, cruel and bitter x 1,000 about Carol being far more interested in another woman than him. But his real issue seems to be his inability to control Carol's life and that her desires and wants don’t lead to him. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>The novel is quite thoughtful and handles the prickliest of subjects with great aplomb. The message is muddled, very much like real life. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The novel is said to be a precursor to Lolita. I can see the influence. Early in the novel I was uncomfortable with the relationship between Therese and Carol. Mostly because Therese is only 19 in the book. She is written as such and it really looks like an adult taking advantage of a young girl with huge mommy issues. And Carol is in the middle of a custody battle for her own daughter. It's not a leap to consider that Therese is a surrogate for Rindy and Carol is more parental than partner. From there it's a very short step to eeewww.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> But we are in skilled hands with Highsmith. She handles this telling the tale through Therese's eyes. The reader views her personal growth. From her childish, romantic view of what love should be to her realization of the hard choices, truths and sacrifices that love requires. She starts out basically an immature girl but by the end of the novel she has grown considerably. The only insight into Carol is through Therese's eyes but there is an increased openness and consideration towards Therese that wasn't there initially. An acknowledgement of maturity and growth. <br><br>Highsmith is masterful and has written a romance novel that is not about love or romance, but about finding and accepting who you are and the strengths needed to live with those truths. Or maybe it was a romance about life and how best to live it. We love who we love. In most cases (there are no absolutes) consenting adults should be able to love each other without judgement. This is a classic. For me, this resembled a sort of LGBT <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37415.Their_Eyes_Were_Watching_God" title="Their Eyes Were Watching God by Zora Neale Hurston" rel="nofollow noopener">Their Eyes Were Watching God</a> with very similar themes about women finding independence and some measure of happiness by taking responsibility for their unconventional choices <i>(disclaimer: Not comparing literary value or oppressions, just themes)</i>. Screw society and its restrictive conventions. Do you!<br><br><i>Almost 4.5 Stars </i><br><br>Read on kindle<br><br><b>Edited to Add:</b> Lowered rating to 4. Upon reflection in comparison to other books and ratings this year, it doesn't outshine many of my 4 star reads.<br><br><i></i></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="10472347455374862238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17998039744343393718">As usual, I feel beneath those who classified this as classic. Once again I feel classic means no spirit, no passion, just a bunch of words to help the reader feel the pain of the author, or characters. Ok, I didn't finish but I felt 50% of the book was more than enough time for me to care about something.<br/><br/>First, I don't believe in the main character who calls herself a New Yorker yet says very little. Not in my experience. And she says she's in love with the other woman but has never asked that </span>
  <span id="freeText17998039744343393718" style="display:none">As usual, I feel beneath those who classified this as classic. Once again I feel classic means no spirit, no passion, just a bunch of words to help the reader feel the pain of the author, or characters. Ok, I didn't finish but I felt 50% of the book was more than enough time for me to care about something.<br /><br />First, I don't believe in the main character who calls herself a New Yorker yet says very little. Not in my experience. And she says she's in love with the other woman but has never asked that woman anything. She knows nothing about the OBJECT of her love.<br /><br />Second, I don't love the other woman, nor respect her. She give nothing of herself.<br /><br />And third, What is up with the caveman? Why does the silent NYer spend any time with this brute?<br /><br />Salt is rubbed into the readers eyes as they try to read this and feel any identification with the author.<br /><br />Sorry. There are billions of good books out there. I will go read one of them.</span>

<div id="review_1490725327" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1490725327" />
    <a title="Vanessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa"><img alt="Vanessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596200358p2/4636758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1490725327?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa" href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa">Vanessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408808979" href="/book/show/10117779-carol">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1490725327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10047993834028629361"><b>4.5 stars.</b> <br/><br/>This was my first Patricia Highsmith book, and I know it won't be my last. Although Highsmith is more known for her suspense writing, <i>Carol</i> is a different style of book altogether. It is part love story, part road novel, and I thoroughly enjoyed the reading experience.<br/><br/>It follows Therese, a young girl working in a department store during the Christmas rush, who one day is enamoured by a customer who comes into her department. Carol is beautiful, in her 30s with a young daughter, and is</span>
  <span id="freeText10047993834028629361" style="display:none"><b>4.5 stars.</b> <br /><br />This was my first Patricia Highsmith book, and I know it won't be my last. Although Highsmith is more known for her suspense writing, <i>Carol</i> is a different style of book altogether. It is part love story, part road novel, and I thoroughly enjoyed the reading experience.<br /><br />It follows Therese, a young girl working in a department store during the Christmas rush, who one day is enamoured by a customer who comes into her department. Carol is beautiful, in her 30s with a young daughter, and is currently going through a difficult divorce. The two women bond and as their relationship deepens, it has a major impact on all other people in their lives.<br /><br />This was a slow-paced book, but I felt like the pace was perfect for the story it told. Each chapter felt sumptuous in its completeness, and always left me satisfied. I was really impressed by Highsmith's writing - there were many beautiful turns of phrase, and I felt like she portrayed a blossoming friendship and love in very believable terms. The characters were fully fleshed out and interesting, and I enjoyed the focus on Therese. Although I found Therese's actions a little selfish at times, I could understand for the most part her reasoning and her frustration at her stilted life in those beginning moments. The road trip aspects of this book were unexpected, as I didn't know much about the book going into it, and felt like I was watching a movie in my head.<br /><br />I am looking forward to watching the movie version, and seeing how it compares to the reading experience. However, if you've not read this book yet, I'd highly recommend picking it up. It is a lovely, captivating read, and a refreshing take on a lesbian relationship than most I have read before.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10047993834028629361" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1318818900" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1318818900" />
    <a title="Aditi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16069171-aditi"><img alt="Aditi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480260251p2/16069171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1318818900?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aditi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aditi" href="/user/show/16069171-aditi">Aditi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393325997" href="/book/show/52258.The_Price_of_Salt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=new-york">new-york</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=road-trip">road-trip</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=friends">friends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=contemporary-romance">contemporary-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=my-reviews">my-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=women-chick-lit">women-chick-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=penguin-books">penguin-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1318818900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer906468645183783591"> <i>“The more you love,the more love you have to give.It's the only feeling we have which is infinite...” </i><br/><br/> ----Christina Westover, <i>an American novelist</i><br/><br/><br/>Patricia Highsmith, an American classic novelist, has penned an incredible tale of love and despair between two same-sex human beings, originally known as <i>The Price of Salt</i>. Later it was re-published with the title, <i>Carol</i>. And in the wake of Supreme Court's decision to legalize same-sex marriage across all the states of USA, I chose to write the revi</span>
  <span id="freeText906468645183783591" style="display:none"> <i>“The more you love,the more love you have to give.It's the only feeling we have which is infinite...” </i><br /><br /> ----Christina Westover, <i>an American novelist</i><br /><br /><br />Patricia Highsmith, an American classic novelist, has penned an incredible tale of love and despair between two same-sex human beings, originally known as <i>The Price of Salt</i>. Later it was re-published with the title, <i>Carol</i>. And in the wake of Supreme Court's decision to legalize same-sex marriage across all the states of USA, I chose to write the review of this beautiful same-sex love story which I read quite a few years ago.<br /><br /><br /><b>Synopsis: </b><br /><br /><i>Therese is just an ordinary sales assistant working in a New York department store when a beautiful, alluring woman in her thirties walks up to her counter. Standing there, Therese is wholly unprepared for the first shock of love. Therese is an awkward nineteen-year-old with a job she hates and a boyfriend she doesn't love; Carol is a sophisticated, bored suburban housewife in the throes of a divorce and a custody battle for her only daughter. As Therese becomes irresistibly drawn into Carol's world, she soon realizes how much they both stand to lose...First published pseudonymously in 1952 as The Price of Salt, Carol is a hauntingly atmospheric love story set against the backdrop of fifties' New York. </i><br /><br /><br />Therese is a 19-year old aspiring theater set designer in New York and has just started working in the department store during the Christmas time to make some extra bucks. And this where Therese meets the most striking and attractive looking older woman named, carol who is in the middle of a divorce battle fighting over the custody of her only daughter. Sparks fly instantly between these two women and gradually their friendship blossoms matures into something blissful and passionate, which leads them to take a vacation together. On learning about Carol's new love-interest, her ex-husband sets a private investigator on their trail to follow Carol so that he can use her "homo-sexuality" reason to win the battle over the custodian of their daughter.<br /><br />I read this book when I was 18 and then I lost touch with the story, then a year ago, I once again re-read this intriguing novel and it certainly made me feel like reading for the very first time, instilling my heart with new passion, and my eyes with new tears. And just like Highsmith's previous novels, this one too has a tone of suspense and thrill through out it's prose, and who better can handle a thriller than Patricia Highsmith- the author who gave birth to the <i>Ripley</i> series and a strong influence to Hitchcock's notable works.<br /><br />The book opens with a slow pace and like most readers, it took time for me to understand the gravity of the storyline. Although, midway through the book, especially the road-trip part where both Therese and Carol goes out on a vacation, intrigued me and that is where the book peaked it's pace and the battle of custody running in the background. The core and the gravity of the story lies in that aggressive battle of custodian. <br /><br />The author has portrayed a modern era through this story where women freely expressed their sexuality and the way they dressed and behaved made more clear that the author is making her story stand on the verge of a new century. <br />The emotions involved in the chemistry of both these women is vividly and intricately laid out with the author's eloquent choice of words.<br /><br />The author, who herself was a bisexual and have had a number of lesbian relationships, have depicted the love between two women and their sexuality with ease and compassion like someone knew what they were dealing with. Although this book is marked as the first every lesbian pulp fiction with a happy-ever-ending, yet the author wrote it under a pseudonym of Claire Morgan, masking her real identity from the original title.<br /><br />Now almost 63 years after it's release, <i>The Price of Salt</i> has been adapted into a movie by the director, Todd Haynes, featuring, Cate Blanchett as Carol Aird and Rooney Mara as Therese Belivet, that will hit the screens by the end of this year.<br /><br /><b>Verdict: </b> <i>Read it before it turns into a motion picture. </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="906468645183783591" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10949615980717344330" style="display:none">so, i read a review of Terry Castle's new book "The Professor" in last Sunday's NYT, and, intrigued, googled her to find out more about her. <br /><br />Many years ago, Castle wrote a book about Les Lit.         <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2016">2016</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1611308392" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6559732611959829871">Reading Highsmith is like a drink of ice cold water - cleansing, refreshing, and invigorating. I noticed the same thing when I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2247142.The_Talented_Mr__Ripley__Ripley___1_' title='The Talented Mr. Ripley (Ripley, #1) by Patricia Highsmith' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Talented Mr. Ripley</a>. Her prose is so spare, so clear. It keeps me focused and skipping along the page. I love it.<br/><br/>Published in 1952, this story was originally written under the pseudonym "Claire Morgan" because of the controversial content - the love story between two women, Therese Beliveau and the older, beautiful and mysterious Carol Aird. It's not terribly su</span>
  <span id="freeText6559732611959829871" style="display:none">Reading Highsmith is like a drink of ice cold water - cleansing, refreshing, and invigorating. I noticed the same thing when I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2247142.The_Talented_Mr__Ripley__Ripley___1_" title="The Talented Mr. Ripley (Ripley, #1) by Patricia Highsmith" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talented Mr. Ripley</a>. Her prose is so spare, so clear. It keeps me focused and skipping along the page. I love it.<br /><br />Published in 1952, this story was originally written under the pseudonym "Claire Morgan" because of the controversial content - the love story between two women, Therese Beliveau and the older, beautiful and mysterious Carol Aird. It's not terribly surprising that this book inspired Nabokov's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7604.Lolita" title="Lolita by Vladimir Nabokov" rel="nofollow noopener">Lolita</a>. Both books are steeped in intoxicating, overwhelming desire, with characters who act on this desire despite societal taboos.<br /><br />By today's standards, the lesbian love story isn't all that shocking. Taken within the 1950's context though, the story is surprisingly frank and timeless. It's about two people navigating their relationship, with all the complications that come with it (not the least being Carol's daughter and very messy divorce). It's full of tension and atmosphere (did I mention the wonderful writing?). It's super stylish - ladies drinking old-fashioneds and highballs - and, the ending is not predictable. <br /><br />Some parts felt a little long, which is why this isn't a 5, but it's pretty darn close.<br /><br />PS There's a great scene I must mention that involves Therese struggling to fly a kite with her boyfriend who she is not attracted to, that English majors would love to dissect as a failing phallus - this was fascinating and entertaining.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6559732611959829871" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('osMno2bZXeTcG47lDtkfhXJkcmJruSmPZPCP/F8c915bkEl/XEyArtOtAL6xwZ1jBbRpWsbRlBVYco1NkshmDw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9uXkGcAL7qkMuP83SKcOMg73Cnm8FXvvDR884rb4lScPtorF+p4z4wMOcWz3v4zUeScRQRF9xnUxnT5TeywEdg==')})
  }

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('bookCover_15677').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
